Comparative assessment of
current and improved solid waste
management system for the
county of Oxfordshire, UK, on an
environmental and economical
basis
A dissertation for the degree of MSc in Environmental Engineering
Author: Ioanna Kyriazi (s172176)
Supervisor: Anders Damgaard, PhD, Senior Researcher
Technical University of Denmark
June 14, 2019
==PAGE:2==
Preface
This document is a Master thesis project of 32.5 ECTS, which is an obligatory re-
quirement for completing the MSc Program of Environmental Engineering at the
Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The MSc thesis project was conducted
from December 2019 to June 2019, excluding the three-week period in January, un-
der the supervision of Senior Researcher Anders Damgaard.
The topic of this study is the solid waste management system of the county of
Oxfordshire, UK, from an environmental and economical point of view and its po-
tential alternatives.
The document comprises of six chapters. Chapter 1 is introductory to the topic
of the thesis and its objectives. Chapter 2 provides background information and
knowledge required for comprehending the content of the thesis.
Chapter 3 de-
scribes the methodologies used for the analyses conducted on the scenarios, while
Chapter 4 presents the results of these analyses. Chapter 5 provides an interpre-
tation of the obtained results. Finally, Chapter 6 presents the conclusions of this
study. Additionally, the Appendices contain detailed information and results.
Acknowledgements
First, I would like to express my gratitude to my supervisor, Anders Damgaard,
for his help and guidance throughout this project. Also, I would like to thank Mr.
Jamie Warmington and Mr. Chris Hoy, from the Waste Modelling team in Ricardo
Energy & Environment, for their willingness to help me, answer my questions and
provide me with valuable information and data.
In addition, I would like to thank my family for their continuous emotional and
ﬁnancial support and their trust and faith in me. Last but not least, I would like to
thank my partner and my friends for their support, patience and guidance through
this journey.

==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Optimised waste management is a key to circular economy and sustainable con-
sumption. The reduction of the amount of waste generated leads to mitigation of
the environmental impacts associated with the production of virgin materials and
the disposal of the waste. This study assessed and compared the waste manage-
ment system of the Oxfordshire county in the United Kingdom for the regularly
collected household waste with two alternative scenarios, in order to identify the
most beneﬁcial scenario from both an environmental and a ﬁnancial perspective.
The methodologies developed for waste Life Cycle Assessment and conventional
Life Cycle Costing were applied, while EASETECH was the software where the sys-
tems were modelled. The attributional modelling framework with system expansion
was followed, in accordance with the goal deﬁnition.
The 258,725 tonnes of waste modelled correspond to the amount collected by the
local authorities during the 2017/18 ﬁnancial year. Data were collected from re-
ports from governmental sources and private contacts, while default processes were
retrieved from the databases of EASETECH and Ecoinvent and were amended to ﬁt
the actual technologies. The ﬁrst scenario modelled, the Baseline scenario, depicted
the current waste management system, while the Deposit Return Scheme scenario
introduced a scheme in the system where citizens would return a fraction of their
plastic bottles, aluminium and steel cans and glass bottles to a Reverse Vending
Machine, from where the materials would be transported to a sorting facility and
then get recycled within the country. In the last scenario, the Separate Collection
scenario, the Baseline scenario was altered by introducing collection of paper, glass,
metals and plastic in separate vehicles. Nearly all the waste collected in this sce-
nario were assumed to be recycled domestically, whereas the recyclables in the other
scenarios were assumed to be exported to Malaysia, depicting the current situation.
The characterised and normalised results were obtained using the method recom-
mended from the ILCD. Based on these results, the most preferable scenario is the
Separate Collection, since it has the lowest impact scores and the greatest beneﬁts
across all the categories, apart from the ozone depletion, where the Baseline sce-
nario performs better. The sensitivity analysis showed that the three scenarios were
particularly sensitive to changes in the electricity mix used for the processes, while
the ratio of recovered electricity aﬀected substantially the results in many impact
categories. Other parameters, such as the substitution ratios of recyclables, were
also found to be sensitive. However, the ranking of the scenarios did not change.
Although the Separate Collection scenario was found to be the most expensive sce-
nario from the local authorities perspective, it could become feasible by cutting the
collection costs, improving the quality of the recyclable materials and introducing
the Extended Produced Responsibility policy. The technical feasibility of the sce-
nario should be assessed before its implementation, by ensuring that the existing
processing facilities have the required capacity.

==PAGE:17==
1
Introduction
1.1
Waste
Waste is deﬁned by the European Commission (2008) as “any substance or object
which the holder discards or intends or is required to discard”. Every human activity
results in waste generation which, depending on the way it is managed, has a speciﬁc
impact on the environment. According to Christensen (2011b), solid waste, which
refers to waste in solid state and sludge from wastewater treatment, is associated
with a list of issues, the extend of which depends on the eﬀectiveness of the waste
management system. First of all, waste takes up space, which especially in densely
populated areas is a problem that cannot be neglected. Apart from this, waste is a
source of nuisances, since it creates unpleasant odours and may attract insects, ro-
dents and other animals, posing a sanitation problem. The pathogens, as well as the
hazardous substances contained in the waste, may spread when easily accessible by
animals. In addition, the several substances that products contain can be released
to the environment, polluting the water, the soil and the air. Despite these threats,
waste has long been a neglected problem in several countries, due to the high man-
agement cost. However, nowadays it is a topic for which awareness is being raised,
especially after the release of the Sustainable Development Goals by the United Na-
tions (2015). The realisation that the resources of the planet are not inﬁnite and
that waste carry a variety of resources, energy and cost has helped in the promotion
of circular economy and sustainable consumption. Reducing the amount of waste
generated leads to avoidance of environmental impacts associated both with the
production of virgin materials and the disposal of the waste (Christensen, 2011b).
Waste is classiﬁed in categories based on their sources and common character-
istics. The main categories are residential, commercial and institutional (C&I), in-
dustrial and construction and demolition waste (CD&E). Residential waste includes
household waste, garden waste, bulky waste and household hazardous waste. Com-
mercial and institutional waste are waste generated in stores, hotels, restaurants,
health care (except health risk waste), banks, insurance companies, education, re-
tirement homes, public services and transport. Industrial waste are waste generated
from industrial production and manufacturing. Finally, construction and demoli-
tion waste is waste from building, repair, remodeling or removal of constructions
(Christensen, 2011a).
1.2
Objectives
In this study, the waste management system of the Oxfordshire county in the United
Kingdom (UK) for the regularly collected household waste will be assessed and com-
pared with several alternative scenarios, in order to minimise the environmental im-
pacts occurring from the system. Consequently, the objectives of this study include
1
==PAGE:18==
the following:
1. Provide background information about the solid waste management system in
England and in Oxfordshire;
2. Provide detailed information regarding the current waste management system
for the collected household waste in Oxfordshire;
3. Conduct a hotspot analysis, where the most impactful processes of the system
will be identiﬁed;
4. Conduct a comparative Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) with alternative scenar-
ios that can minimise the environmental impacts of the system and improve
compliance with the legislation and the strategies;
5. Conduct a ﬁnancial Life Cycle Costing (LCC), where the most important costs
and revenues will be identiﬁed and the feasibility of the scenarios will be tested;
6. Identify the most beneﬁcial scenario from both an environmental and a ﬁnan-
cial perspective;
7. Suggest future improvements beyond the scenarios that were analysed.
2
==PAGE:19==
2
Background
This chapter contains knowledge on which this study is based and which a reader
needs in order to fully understand the content of this study.
2.1
Legislation
In 1999, the European Commission (EC) set technical and operational requirements
for the landﬁlls, in order to minimise their environmental impact, and suggested
further diversion of waste from the landﬁlls. Also, it established a set of targets, the
latest of which state that by 2020 the Member States shall reduce the amount of
biodegradable municipal waste going to landﬁlls by 35% of that produced in 1995
(European Commission, 1999).
Furthermore, the revised Waste Framework Directive (European Commission,
2008) is currently the predominant legal instrument, on which all the Member States
rely on to form their national policy and legislation. In this directive, several terms
and concepts regarding waste and waste management are deﬁned. It also introduces
the Waste hierarchy, a concept which prioritises the waste management options
with the following order: prevention, preparing for reuse, recycling, other recovery
(e.g. energy recovery) and disposal. It is suggested that this order is followed in
order to achieve the best environmental performance, but deviation is accepted if
it is justiﬁed by life-cycle thinking. Additionally, the Directive established some
fundamental principles. First, it is a requirement that waste management is con-
ducted protecting the human health and the environment. Second, it states that:
1) the cost of the management should be borne by the original waste producer or by
the current or previous holders (polluter-pays principle), 2) a network of facilities
shall be designed in order to make the Community self-suﬃcient in terms of waste
disposal (self-suﬃciency principle), 3) the waste should be treated in the nearest
appropriate facility (proximity principle) and 4) any natural or legal person who
professionally develops, manufactures, processes, treats, sells or imports products is
responsible for it as waste (Extended Producer Responsibility (EPR)). In addition,
separate collection should take place for at least paper, glass, plastic and metal by
2015 if technically, environmentally and economically practical (TEEP) and reuse
or recycle at least 50% by weight of them in Municipal Solid Waste (MSW) by 2020.
In the UK, the waste policy is deﬁned by each country independently. The
Waste Framework Directive was implemented as national law in the “England, The
Waste (England and Wales) Regulations 2011” (as amended) (SI 2011/127, 2011).
In 2014, the “Environmental Permitting (England and Wales) (Amendment) Reg-
ulations” were issued (SI 2014/255, 2014), requiring the inputs and outputs of the
Material Recovery Facilities (MRFs) to be monitored and published. Also, in 2017
the “Producer Responsibility Obligations (Packaging Waste) (Amendment) Regula-
tions” (SI 2017/1221, 2017) set new recycling targets for glass, plastic, aluminium,
steel, paper and wood. Apart from these regulations, several policies that compli-
3
==PAGE:20==
ment the waste strategy have been issued. As guidance to the separate collection in-
cluded in the Waste Framework Directive, the “Waste Regulations Route Map” was
issued to assist the local authorities, since the term ‘technically, environmentally and
economically practicable’ is not quite clear in practice (WRAP, 2014). The “Greater
Consistency framework” was also published by the Waste & Resources Action Pro-
gramme (WRAP, 2017) to facilitate household recycling in England by intervening
in several levels. Last but not least, the new waste and resources strategy for Eng-
land was published by the Department for Environment, Food and Rural Aﬀairs
in December 2018 (DEFRA, 2018d), setting new targets and analysing important
issues, together with the 25 year plan published earlier the same year (DEFRA,
2018a). These two last documents will be described in detail in 2.3.
2.2
Solid Waste Management
Solid waste management is linked with a series of environmental impacts. A few
mentioned by Laurent et al. (2014a) are climate change, due to greenhouse gases
(GHGs) emitted from landﬁlls, human health risks, due to exposure to hazards dur-
ing collection and treatment, resource depletion, due to lack of recycling of valuable
elements, and ecosystem damages, due to emissions of heavy metals. The target
when a waste management plan is set is to reduce as much as possible these impacts
and contribute to a more environmentally sustainable society.
While designing a waste management plan, it is required that eﬀort is put into
handling the waste with the most eﬀective way for each single case, since it is a
complex problem that demands a lot of time and the contribution of all the relevant
actors. Also, it is important that the plan follows the national and local strategy,
contributing to the achievement of the set targets. One of the challenges the plan-
ners face is the management of new materials, for which no end-of-life options are
already designed (Ragossnig and Tunesi, 2018).
2.3
Waste management in England
DEFRA (2019) reports that, in 2016, 222.9 million tonnes of waste were generated in
the UK, 85% of which were generated in England. 48.5% of this amount was treated
with ‘recycling or other recovery’, while 24.4% was landﬁlled. As seen in Figure 1,
the largest amount of waste was generated by the Construction, Demolition and
Excavation (CD&E) sector, while 18% of the total was generated by the commercial
and industrial sector. Households accounted for 12% of the waste, while ‘Other’,
which consists of waste from mining, agriculture, forestry and ﬁshing, accounted for
6%.
The methods with which the generated waste were treated are illustrated in
Figure 2. Most of the waste were recycled, composted or anaerobically digested,
while one fourth of them were landﬁlled. Other treatment methods included energy
4
==PAGE:21==
Figure 1: Waste generated in England in 2016, split by source
recovery (incineration with energy recovery and R1 accreditation), backﬁlling, in-
cineration and land treatment and release into water bodies.
Household waste are managed by the local authorities in the UK, while for
Figure 2: Waste treatment in England, 2016
other waste the producers are responsible. DEFRA (2018a) mentions that approxi-
mately 86% of the waste managed by local authorities is household waste, while the
rest is coming from street cleaning, parks and grounds, business and construction.
It is worth mentioning that, in the UK, the term ‘Waste from Households (WfH)’ is
used to refer to household waste. WfH includes waste from regular household collec-
tion, civic amenity sites, bulky waste and ‘other household waste’, while it excludes
waste from street cleaning/sweeping, gully emptying, separately collected healthcare
waste, soil, rubble, plasterboard and asbestos waste (DEFRA, 2019). However, in
this study, the term household waste will be used when referring to WfH. The WfH
5
==PAGE:22==
had a 44.8% recycling rate in 2017/18 ﬁnancial year in England (DEFRA, 2018b).
A total of 22,225 thousand tonnes were collected by the Local Authorities, which
corresponds to 400 kg/person. The residual waste stream, including rejects from
recycling, constituted the 55% of the waste, while separately collected food waste
accounted for only 2%.
One of the waste problems that is widely discussed is the food waste. It is
estimated that, in the UK, 10 million tonnes of food and drink are wasted each year
after the farm gate, which corresponds to a ﬁnancial cost of £20 billion (DEFRA,
2018d). One ﬁfth of the UK carbon emissions are estimated to be attributed to the
consumption of food and drinks. Consequently, the government aims at reducing
the food waste by half by 2030, meeting the UN Sustainable Development Goal 12.3.
In the 25 Year Plan issued by DEFRA (2018a), a goal of zero avoidable waste
by 2050 and zero avoidable plastic waste by 2042 is set, together with the aim to stop
exporting waste to other countries. Furthermore, in the “Our waste, our resources:
a strategy for England” document (DEFRA, 2018d), a more detailed strategy to-
wards a better management of the waste is analysed. The objectives of the strategy
refer to the waste minimisation, the increase of resource eﬃciency and the plan to-
wards a circular economy, while controlling and eliminating the damage caused to
the environment by managing waste safely and carefully. Additional goals to the
ones set in the 25 Year Plan are the ambition to make all plastic packaging placed
on the market recyclable, reusable or compostable by 2025, divert food waste from
landﬁlls by 2030 and double resource productivity by 2050.
The strategy refers to several issues and targets regarding recycling, which
should be at least 65% of municipal waste by weight by 2035, decreasing the per-
centage of landﬁlling to 10%. First, more clear and easy guidelines regarding recy-
clable objects and materials and their corresponding bins should be in place. An
important step towards the aforementioned goal is the implementation of a Deposit
Return Scheme (DRS), where consumers are charged with a deposit when they buy
single-use containers, which they retrieve when they return the container to the de-
signed facilities. As already proven in countries where the scheme is implemented,
a DRS leads to higher eﬃciencies both for the collection and the material recovery
than the separate collection scheme (Bisinella et al., 2018). Also, eﬀort will be put
in supporting investments in domestic recycling of materials, since the current sys-
tem seems to lead to export of the packaging waste, increasing the environmental
impacts of the treatment. Additionally, impact-based targets will be used in the
future instead of the weight-based ones, since this basis can make leaders neglect
lightweight materials, such as plastics.
Furthermore, the new EPR will demand from producers to be more respon-
sible for the products they release, starting with Waste Electrical and Electronic
Equipment (WEEE) in 2020. Also, it is a requirement that the incineration plants
become more eﬃcient, by utilising the heat produced and building district heat
networks where possible.
6
==PAGE:23==
2.4
Life Cycle Assessment
Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) is a tool that quantiﬁes the environmental impacts of
a product or product system over its entire life cycle (i.e. from ‘cradle to grave’),
from raw materials extraction through production and manufacturing, use, end of
life treatment and disposal (ISO, 2006). A LCA can be used to identify ways to
improve the environmental performance of a product, to inform decision-makers and
to market a product, by fulﬁlling ecolabelling criteria or producing an environmen-
tal product declaration. The beneﬁts from the implementation of LCA in various
applications lie on the fact that it takes a life cycle perspective, preventing burden
shifting, it covers multiple environmental issues, assigns numbers to facts, facilitat-
ing comparison of diﬀerent processes and product systems, and is based on science
(Hauschild et al., 2018). However, due to the amount of data and time required
to complete a LCA, simpliﬁcations and generalisations are often required, which
increase the uncertainty of the results. Also, the risk of rare events, such as natural
disasters, as well as the social or economic impacts are not considered.
The International Organization for Standardization (ISO) has published two
frameworks that specify the methodology that a LCA should follow, ISO 14040 and
ISO 14044 (ISO, 2006). According to these, a LCA is an iterative process which
comprises of four phases: the goal and scope deﬁnition, the inventory analysis, the
impact assessment and the interpretation.
In the goal and scope deﬁnition, the
reasons why the study is carried out, the intended applications, the functional unit,
the system boundaries and data quality requirements are deﬁned. During the sec-
ond phase, data regarding the inputs and the outputs of the modelled system are
collected. The Life Cycle Impact Assessment (LCIA) phase quantiﬁes the environ-
mental impacts by assigning Life Cycle Inventory (LCI) data to impact categories
(classiﬁcation) using characterisation factors to assess their contribution (character-
isation). Also, during this phase, two additional steps may take place, normalisation
and weighting. Normalisation is used to put the results in a perspective, using a
common reference unit. Weighting allows the comparison across impact categories,
by grouping them and assigning weighting factors according to the perceived sever-
ity. Finally, in the interpretation phase, the results are summarised and discussed
in accordance with the goal and scope of the study. In this step, a sensitivity and
uncertainty analysis is conducted, in order to evaluate the robustness of the results
and identify the limitations of the study.
2.4.1
Waste Life Cycle Assessment
LCA is recommended in the Waste Framework Directive (European Commission,
2008) for the comparison of waste management options. According to Laurent et al.
(2014a), LCA can contribute in the identiﬁcation of the appropriate waste man-
agement solutions.
Waste LCA is deﬁned as the LCA that is applied to waste
management systems, as only the end-of-life phase of the product’s life cycle is in-
7
==PAGE:24==
cluded, as shown in Figure 3. Since only the treatment options are assessed, it is
usual to omit the previous life cycle stages, which is also known as the “zero-burden
assumption” (Hauschild et al., 2018). The ﬁrst applications of waste LCAs started
in the early 1990s, while during the past years the interest in using this method
to develop and analyse waste management strategies has been increased (Björklund
et al., 2010).
The beneﬁts of using LCA as a methodology to analyse waste management
Figure 3: The life cycle of a product (DEFRA, 2018d)
systems lies on the fact that it breaks down their complexity and provides a compre-
hensive view of the contributing processes and parameters (Björklund et al., 2010).
Figure 4 shows the compartments that are usually included in waste LCAs. Apart
from the waste management itself, that consists of collecting and treating the waste,
several products may be produced, such as electricity, heat and recovered materials.
In this case, the system boundaries are expanded, in order to include the avoided
burdens of recovering resources instead of producing virgin materials and energy
(Björklund et al., 2010).
A factor that needs to be considered when modelling the substitution of virgin
materials, which is the result of the recycling processes, is the substitution ratio.
The substitution ratio (SubR) is calculated by multiplying the technological eﬃ-
ciency (A) with the market response (B). The technological eﬃciency represents
the eﬃciency of the recovering process by estimating the material losses in the ac-
tivity, while the market response estimates the loss of material grade or quality of
the secondary material produced (DTU Environment, 2018b). The substitution ra-
tio depends on the technology used, but it is also inherent of the type of material
recycled (Gentil et al., 2010). As Laurent et al. (2014b) mention, overestimating
the substitution ratio results in increased beneﬁts from the recycling processes and,
consequently, aﬀects signiﬁcantly the ﬁnal results of a LCA.
8
==PAGE:25==
Figure 4: The system boundaries of waste LCAs (Björklund et al., 2010)
Furthermore, Tunesi (2011) points out the need for increased geographical res-
olution when waste LCAs compare waste management strategies. By considering
the local conditions and limitations, the impacts can be assessed more realistically.
However, this focus requires speciﬁc data regarding waste generation and the facili-
ties, which are not always available or easy to have access to.
2.4.1.1
Waste LCA modelling tools
Waste LCAs are conducted either by using tools developed for this purpose or by
using product LCA tools (Gentil et al., 2010).
Several waste LCA models have
been developed, such as IWM, ORWARE, WISARD, WRATE and EASETECH
(Björklund et al., 2010). Gentil et al. (2010) assessed eight diﬀerent models, con-
cluding that discrepancies in the results occur, due to modelling assumptions and,
mostly, because of the time and the country the models were developed. Björklund
et al. (2010) also mention that models may diﬀer because of alterations in objec-
tives, intended users or the waste management policy of the countries where they
are developed.
The Waste and Resources Assessment Tool for the Environment (WRATE) was
developed on behalf of the Environment Agency for England and Wales, the Scottish
Environment Protection Agency and the Department of Environment in Northern
Ireland, in order to provide a model for the management of municipal solid waste.
Some default data are already implemented in the model to assist the users. These
default databases include 160 waste management technologies datasets and the en-
ergy mix for 40 countries, both average and marginal, over a 20-year forecast. It
also includes a database on materials and their inventories (Ecoinvent database),
9
==PAGE:26==
a default waste composition, which is the national UK waste composition, and the
most commonly applied impact assessment methods. The waste management tech-
nologies are based on 12 process stages including construction, maintenance and
decommissioning inputs and outputs, energy (fuel, electricity, gas), materials and
water inputs. The outputs are deﬁned by the emissions to air, water and soil. The
outputs are also deﬁned by the production of energy, in the form of heat and elec-
tricity, and new materials, in the form of compost and recyclable materials. The life
cycle scope of WRATE is deﬁned as ‘gate to grave’ (Björklund et al., 2010).
WRATE is the most widely applied waste LCA modelling tool in the UK. How-
ever, several issues have been identiﬁed. First, the characteristics of the waste, as
well as its elemental composition cannot be modiﬁed. These data are predeﬁned
and are based on a study conducted in the UK for household waste from 1991 until
1994. Secondly, the model handles multifunctional processes with allocation, which
according to ISO (2006) is the least preferred approach. Also, the cost of this model
is signiﬁcant (Turner, 2016).
The Environmental Assessment System for Environmental Technologies
(EASETECH), a model developed as an updated second version of EASEWASTE,
is a waste LCA modelling tool developed by the Technical University of Denmark
(DTU) (Clavreul et al., 2014). It is a ﬂexible and adjustable tool, while still allowing
complex modelling. All the default data included can be modiﬁed by the user and
processes and data can be imported from databases, such as Ecoinvent. Several
LCIA methods are included, such as ReCiPe, ILCD, USEtox, and new ones that are
implemented during the updates or can be created manually by the user. Tools for
the uncertainty and sensitivity analysis are also available in the model. Additionally,
when used for academic purposes, EASETECH does not require a license fee. Al-
though the drawbacks of using WRATE to assess the environmental impacts of solid
waste management systems are not present when using EASETECH, according to
Turner (2016), there are two issues when applying the model to UK cases. First, the
geographical scope of the assessment may be compromised, since EASETECH uses
data speciﬁc to Danish and northern European technologies and conditions, which
may though be modiﬁed by the user, solving this issue. Second, a training course
at DTU is required for its use.
2.5
Life Cycle Costing (LCC)
According to JRC-IES (2011), Life Cycle Costing (LCC) is an approach to estab-
lish the cost of diﬀerent options over their entire life cycles, which is deﬁned as the
present value of the costs, negative or positive, necessary to fulﬁl the functional unit.
Examples of relevant costs are investment costs, operative costs and sales revenues,
which are considered negative costs.
LCC can be used to analyse the economic
eﬀects of a LCA system, using the same system boundaries and modelling as the
LCA. The goal of the analysis may be the support to the planning or optimisation
process, the evaluation of investment decisions or a hotspot identiﬁcation as part of
a life cycle sustainability assessment, which considers the environmental, economic
10
==PAGE:27==
and social impact of a product (Hauschild et al., 2018).
There are three types of LCC: conventional LCC (cLCC), environmental LCC
(eLCC) and societal LCC (sLCC). With the ﬁrst type, also called ﬁnancial LCC, all
life cycle costs that are directly covered by the main producer or user in the product
life cycle are assessed. In the second type, the costs are linked with the functional
unit and all the costs that are covered by all stakeholders are assessed. Additionally,
eLCC may include external costs that could be internalised in the near future, such
as a tax on plastics. Societal LCC is the most advanced type of LCC, since it also
quantiﬁes the environmental and social impacts in monetary terms. This type of
LCC incorporates the LCA results, assigning costs to potential damages that the
product system may cause, such as health hazards.
In this study, a conventional LCC will be conducted, simply called LCC through-
out the analysis. The result of a cLCC is calculated from the sum of the budget
costs and transfers for the activities included in a solid waste management system.
As Martinez Sanchez (2016) mentions, cLCCs are used by researchers and others
involved in the waste management sector in order to assess the economic feasibility
of treatment solutions, to identify the most economical option and to evaluate the
economic consequences of implementing a speciﬁc waste solution.
2.6
Literature review
While waste LCAs are conducted in the UK, due to the development of the models,
local LCAs are scarce in the literature. It is usual to conduct partial LCAs, carbon
footprints and studies only for one waste fraction (food, paper) or only one technol-
ogy (incineration, AD).
In England, several LCAs have been conducted. Papageorgiou et al. (2009)
performed a carbon footprint study on technologies used for energy recovery from
municipal waste. The technologies assessed were the incineration with energy recov-
ery, the mechanical biological treatment and the mechanical heat treatment, while
it was concluded that, for the last two treatment options, the market for the fuel
produced is essential for their emissions, but, when combined with incineration with
electricity and heat recovery for the residues, the carbon emissions are minimised.
Tunesi (2011) conducted a partial LCA assessing three impact categories to compare
alternative waste management scenarios for energy recovery in England, underlining
the importance of ﬂexibility between local and regional management options. Al-
Salem et al. (2014) completed a LCA assessing four impact categories. The study
assessed the impacts of the current waste management system in the Greater Lon-
don area and the impacts of two alternative thermochemical treatment technologies
for the management of plastics.
In UK level, Tonini et al. (2018) conducted a LCA study for the avoidable food
waste generated by processing, wholesale and retail, food service and households.
Ten environmental impact categories were considered. The estimated carbon foot-
print ranged from 2,000 to 3,600 kg CO2-eq/tonne, depending from the composition
of the waste. It was also mentioned that anaerobic digestion and incineration pro-
11
==PAGE:28==
vided savings when replacing landﬁlling. Jeswani and Azapagic (2016) undertook a
LCA to compare the environmental impacts from recovering energy from incinera-
tion or landﬁll gas. It was concluded that the incineration had much lower impacts
for all the categories except for human toxicity. Also, it was estimated that, if all
the MSW were incinerated instead of landﬁlled, 2.3% of the UK electricity demand
would be covered and 0.3-0.45% of national GHG emissions would be saved. Bassi
et al. (2017) also conducted a LCA for household waste generated in seven countries,
including the UK, showing the importance of the value of recovered materials and
energy substitute, as well as the minimisation of environmental impacts by avoid-
ing landﬁlling.
Turner (2016) conducted a carbon footprint study for the waste
management sector, showing the inconsistency and the lack of transparency by the
companies reporting their emissions. Also, he conducted a carbon footprint study
for the waste collected by the local authorities in Cardiﬀ, where landﬁlling was iden-
tiﬁed as the main contributor.
Other relevant studies were also found in the literature. Iriarte et al. (2009)
assessed the environmental impacts of three waste collection systems, the mobile
pneumatic, the multi-container and the door-to-door, in densely populated urban
areas. The results showed that the multi-container scheme had the lowest impacts,
while intercity transport is an important stage. Turconi et al. (2011) conducted a
LCA for the waste incineration in Denmark and Italy using two diﬀerent models,
SimaPro and EASEWASTE. The Danish system performed better, mainly because
of the heat recovery at the plant. Other factors that inﬂuenced the results were
the waste composition, the ﬂue gas cleaning system and the treatment of the solid
residues. Diﬀerences between the results of the two models were observed, but the
ranking of the scenarios remained the same. Another study, conducted by Belboom
et al. (2013), compared technologies for the management of MSW in Liege, Bel-
gium. The way the environmental impacts are minimised by replacing landﬁlling
with incineration with energy recovery was shown.
12
==PAGE:29==
3
Methodology
During the literature review (2.6), no LCA assessing the environmental impacts of
the waste management within a county was found. Some studies are conducted for
large cities in the UK, such as London and Cardiﬀ, while other concentrate on speciﬁc
technologies. A smaller level LCA can show with greater detail the problematic
processes and materials (Tunesi, 2011). Also, by using site-speciﬁc data regarding
the waste generation and the facilities, the results will be more accurate, allowing
the local council to plan future improvements. By introducing several scenarios, it
will be possible to ﬁnd a realistic way to achieve the targets set by the government
and the European Commission.
3.1
Waste management in Oxfordshire
Oxfordshire is a county in South East England with an estimated population of
682,444 residents in 248,000 households. The county comprises of ﬁve districts: the
city of Oxford, the Cherwell District, the South Oxfordshire District, the Vale of
White Horse District and the West Oxfordshire District in an area of 260,494 ha
(WasteDataFlow, 2019). During the ﬁnancial year 2017/18, the local authorities
collected 313,442 tonnes of waste, 92% of which were household waste (DEFRA,
2018c).
That amount corresponded to 419 kg/person, which is higher than the
average in England. However, Oxfordshire is one of the counties with the highest
recycling rates, managing to recycle, reuse or compost 57% of the household waste,
while landﬁlling 5%. Also, 37% of the household waste were incinerated at the Ard-
ley Energy Recovery Facility, which began operation in 2014.
The Oxfordshire Waste Partnership (2013) stated that their vision is “A soci-
ety where everyone tries to prevent waste and sees waste materials as a potential
resource”.
Apart from achieving a high recycling rate in comparison with other
counties, the Partnership committed to incorporating carbon footprint studies to
facilitate the decision making process, while reducing the carbon emissions by 50%
of 2008 levels by 2030. Finally, a target of 70% recycling of household waste by 2031
was set in 2015 (Oxfordshire County Council, 2015).
3.2
Goal & Scope deﬁnition
This study aims at providing the Oxfordshire City Council with a valuable insight
on the points where the waste management system for household waste in Oxford-
shire performs poorly environmentally. Suggested improvements are to be presented
and assessed from a life-cycle perspective, both from an environmental and ﬁnancial
point of view. The LCA only includes household waste generated within Oxfordshire
and collected from the local authorities via regular household collection, disregarding
13
==PAGE:30==
waste from bring sites and Civic Amenity sites, bulky and other household waste.
The reasons for selecting this source of waste lie on the fact that household waste
has a complex composition with plenty of material fractions, such as paper, organ-
ics, several kinds of plastic, hazardous waste, glass and metals (Turner, 2016). Also,
the sites are disregarded because there seems to be little room for improvement in
comparison with the collected waste. Consequently, the focus on this source of waste
is one factor that limits the application of this study on generic cases. The decision
context of this LCA is speciﬁed as “A: micro-level decision support”, since the de-
cisions that may result from it are not expected to aﬀect signiﬁcantly the market
for virgin materials or the electricity mix of England. More details regarding the
goal deﬁnition can be found in Appendix A, while the terms are explained in the
Glossary.
The functional unit of the system is deﬁned as the management of the household
waste generated in the county of Oxfordshire and collected from the local authorities
during the the 2017/18 ﬁnancial year (01/04/2017-31/03/2018), in accordance with
the European and English legislation, the most important of which are the Waste
Framework Directive (European Commission, 2008) and the Waste (England and
Wales) Regulations 2011 (SI 2011/127, 2011). The amount corresponding to this
statement, also known as reference ﬂow in LCA terms, is 258,725 tonnes (Waste-
DataFlow, 2019). Also, the composition of this amount is speciﬁed in Table 1, as
derived from data on WasteDataFlow (2019) and conﬁdential data.

Since the decision context is speciﬁed as A, the chosen LCI modelling framework
is the attributional with system expansion, as suggested in the ISO 14044 standard
(ISO, 2006) and the ILCD guidelines (ILCD, 2010). Thus, speciﬁc data are used for
14
==PAGE:31==
the processes in the foreground system, while average data are used for the processes
in the background system.
In order to better deﬁne the system and present the processes that are included
in it, the system boundaries are illustrated in Figure 5. The dashed line shows the
boundary between the background and the foreground system. The boxes in the
background system show the inputs that are required from the system and the out-
puts that result from it, while the arrows indicate the waste ﬂows. The dotted arrows
show the avoided production of materials or energy in the background system. The
household waste generated are collected and transported in three diﬀerent fractions,
the organics, the residuals and the recyclables. The organic fraction is transported
to a biological treatment facility, which may be either an Anaerobic Digestion (AD)
plant or a composting plant. Its treatment results in the production of electricity
and heat and the formation of either digestate or compost. The digestate or the
compost are credited for substituting conventional fertilisers, while the electricity
and the heat are credited for the corresponding amount the process provides. The
recyclables are transported to a MRF, where a certain amount gets recycled and,
thus, substitutes virgin materials, while the rest, which does not fulﬁl the require-
ments for recycling, are transported to a facility, which may be a landﬁll, a RDF
facility or an Energy Recovery Facility (ERF). The Refuse Derived Fuel (RDF), after
its formation, is incinerated in the ERF. Finally, the residuals are transported to an
ERF or a landﬁll, where the incineration produces electricity, heat, bottom ash and
ﬂy ash and the landﬁll produces electricity. The bottom ash substitutes primary
or secondary aggregates, the electricity and heat substitute a certain amount in the
grid and the ﬂy ash is landﬁlled. Transport is included in this study, since in the case
of waste LCAs, it can contribute signiﬁcantly to the environmental impacts (Clift
et al., 2000). Also, the capital goods are included in the system boundaries, since,
as Brogaard and Christensen (2016) concluded, they contribute signiﬁcantly in the
environmental impact of waste management systems. However, in this study exist-
ing infrastructure is used and, thus, capital goods are not expected to contribute
signiﬁcantly (more than 5%).
15
==PAGE:32==
Figure 5: System boundaries
16
==PAGE:33==
The processes in this LCA are modelled speciﬁcally for the county of Oxfordshire.
Since data are not always available for small regions, data generated for England are
used in some cases. Also, processes modelled for Northern Europe can be representa-
tive, due to similar technology and weather conditions. However, since technologies
and data linked with waste management can be outdated rapidly, it is expected that
this study can be representative for ﬁve years and eﬀort is put in retrieving data no
older than ﬁve years.
Aiming at presenting a complete assessment of the environmental impacts of
the system, all the impact categories are presented in the results. The categories are:
climate change (kg CO2-eq), ozone depletion (kg CFC-11-eq), human toxicity (can-
cer and non-cancer eﬀects, CTUh), particulate matter (PM2.5-eq), ionising radiation
(kBq U235-eq), photochemical ozone formation (kg NMVOC), terrestrial acidiﬁca-
tion (mol H+-eq), terrestrial eutrophication (mol N-eq), freshwater eutrophication
(kg P-eq), marine eutrophication (kg N-eq), freshwater ecotoxicity (CTUe), deple-
tion of fossil abiotic resources (MJ) and depletion of elements (kg antimony-eq),
as recommended by EC-JRC (2011). The model used is EASETECH and the Life
Cycle Impact Assessment (LCIA) method is the “ILCD recommended 2013 Prosuit
Global NR w/o LT”. More details regarding the scope deﬁnition can be found in
Appendix B, while the terms are explained in the Glossary.
3.3
Scenarios
3.3.1
Data Collection
The main source of data for the modelling was the reported data from the local
authorities retrieved from WasteDataFlow (2019). DEFRA and WRAP reports were
used to identify the most common processes used in England, while contacts with
the private sector were made to obtain more accurate knowledge on the local waste
management systems. Where the aforementioned data sources were not suﬃcient,
scientiﬁc literature was used. Most of the processes used were found in EASETECH
v.3.1.0 and some were obtained from the Ecoinvent 3.5 database. The data sources
are documented in detail in Tables 53, 54 and 55, Appendix C.2, where also their
quality is discussed.
For the LCC, the gate fees, that can be found in Appendix D ,Table 56, were
retrieved from WRAP (2018). The prices for recyclables materials, including their
transport (ex-works), were found on letsrecycle.com (2019) for March 2018 and can
be seen in Appendix D, Table 57. The costs of collection, including staﬀsalaries,
truck maintenance and number of vehicles and workers required, as well as capital
costs, such as the price for a Reverse Vending Machine (RVM) and new bins, were
obtained through communication with the private sector. The income of the district
councils from the charge for the collection of garden waste was calculated using prices
reported from the Oxfordshire County Council (2019).
17
==PAGE:34==
3.3.2
LCI Data
3.3.2.1
Baseline scenario
The Baseline scenario, illustrated in Figure 6, depicts the current waste manage-
ment system. The composition of the waste entering the system was retrieved from
WasteDataFlow (2019). The sources were split into multi and single-family house-
holds, depicting the diﬀerent waste composition and generation rates between the
rural and the urban area. The speciﬁc composition for both areas can be found in
Tables 36, 37, 38 and 39 in Appendix C. The waste is segregated into four streams:
food, garden, recyclable and residual waste. These streams are collected with dif-
ferent types of collection trucks. Food waste is collected in a truck with a capacity
of 5.7 tonnes, while for the other streams the capacity is 14 tonnes. According to
Turner (2016), a distance of 30 km for a round trip in rural areas is representa-
tive. On EASETECH, collection processes in cities need on average 50% less diesel
and, thus, it was assumed that the distance travelled for the collection in the city
of Oxford is 50% less than the distance travelled in the rest of the county. The
distances needed for the trucks to reach the corresponding facilities are included in
the modelled distances for all the waste apart from the residual waste, for which the
process “Transport to MRF” is modelled to show the transport of the waste to the
facility. The distances for the waste streams are presented in Table 2.
Table 2: Distances travelled for each waste stream

Food waste, apart from the food waste collected in the Cherwell District, are
directed to the Anaerobic Digestion Facility, where it is transformed into energy and
digestate. The electricity produced from the biogas is released into the electricity
grid, the heat is used on site for the needs of the process, while the digestate, which
is rich in nutrients, is applied on nearby ﬁelds. The use of the digestate as fertiliser
results in not using other conventional fertilisers. Garden waste, apart from the
garden waste collected in the Cherwell District, are directed to the Open Windrow
Composting facility, where, after the required time, a compost is formed, which is
also used on land, substituting mineral fertilisers. Rejects from this process, such
as wood chips, are sent to the ERF. The organic waste collected from the Cherwell
District are directed to the In-Vessel Composting (IVC) facility, where, in a similar
but more sophisticated process, a compost is produced. This compost follows the
18
==PAGE:35==
same process described above. Rejects from this process are sent to the ERF.
The recyclable materials are transported for 145 km to a MRF. This distance
is the average distance of the distances from the MRFs reported on WasteDataFlow
(2019). The recyclables are sorted into paper, card, mixed paper and card, glass,
plastics, aluminium and steel. The sorting eﬃciencies are based on the reported
eﬃciencies by WRAP (2019) for the facility where most of the waste of the county
are sent, in Edmonton, London (Table 47, Appendix C.1). Glass, aluminium and
steel were assumed to be recycled in the UK. An average distance of 160 km is
believed to be representative for these materials to reach recycling facilities. The
recycling processes used were found in EASETECH, where the electricity source was
changed to the electricity mix of UK (see Table 31, Appendix C), to be in accordance
with the modelling framework of this study. The eﬃciency of the recycling process,
represented from the substitution ratio as described in 2.4.1, the material that was
substituted, as well as the treatment process that was followed for the residues are
presented in Table 3 for all the materials that were recycled.

The other material fractions are recycled both in the UK and in Malaysia, which
was assumed to be the destination according to WRAP (2015). The proportion be-
tween the domestic and the exported recycling was based on data and, partly, on
assumptions. 30% of the paper is recycled in the UK, while the remaining 70% is
shipped to Malaysia, travelling a distance of 14,910 km. 45% of the plastics are recy-
19
==PAGE:36==
cled in the UK and the other 55% is sent to Malaysia, where it is recycled following
the same process. 20% of the card is recycled within the UK. The remaining 80% is
recycled in Malaysia using the same process, with the only diﬀerence found in the
management of the residues, which are landﬁlled. This diﬀerence is also occurring
in the recycling process of the mixed paper & card fraction, 90% of which is recycled
in paper mills in the UK.
The rejects that result from the sorting process in the MRF are either land-
ﬁlled, incinerated or processed into RDF. The ﬁnal destinations are reported on
WasteDataFlow (2019), but speciﬁc material fractions are assumed to be directed
to speciﬁc facilities (see Table 48, Appendix C.1). The rejects processed to RDF
are transported to an exporting facility, which is 46 km away, according to Waste-
DataFlow (2019). From there, it is shipped to Sweden, where it is incinerated in a
ERF in the city of Västerås. The facility was modelled based on the process already
existing on EASETECH. The electricity produced substitutes electricity from the
grid (see Table 33, Appendix C, for electricity mix), while the heat substitutes heat
produced from natural gas. The Air Pollution Control (APC) residues, also referred
to as ﬂy ash, is assumed to be used as backﬁlling material in old mines, while the
Incinerator Bottom Ash (IBA) is assumed to be used as aggregate for road construc-
tion. Metals found in the IBA are recycled to new steel or aluminium sheets with
the eﬃciencies presented in Table 3.
The residual waste collected in the county are managed locally. 9.9% is land-
ﬁlled in a nearby landﬁll, where the landﬁll gas that is generated from the decom-
posing waste is collected with an average eﬃciency of 58% over the 100 years of
the process and combusted, producing electricity with an eﬃciency of 25% that is
released into the grid. The remaining 90.1%, together with the residues from the
biological treatment and the MRF, are incinerated in the Ardley ERF. Electricity
produced from the process with an eﬃciency of 27% is released into the grid. How-
ever, the heat is not utilised, since there is no heating network. The APC residues,
the IBA and the metals from the IBA are managed in the same way as in the Swedish
facility.
The speciﬁc locations of the facilities used can be found in Table 35, Appendix
C. Although each local authority reported diﬀerent facilities, in order to make this
project feasible, only one facility was assumed to be used from each type. The facil-
ity used in the modelling process was selected based either on the accepted volume
or on its representativeness. Since all the scenarios were assumed to use existing in-
frastructure and taking into consideration the comparative nature of this study, the
capital goods associated with the systems were not modelled. Details regarding the
processes that were modelled on EASETECH can be found in Appendix C, Table 40.

3.3.2.2
Deposit Return Scheme (DRS) scenario
The second scenario, called DRS scenario, introduces a Deposit Return Scheme
(DRS) to the waste management system. The citizens segregate their recyclable
waste into one more stream, which contains metal cans, plastic and glass bottles.
The ratio between the materials going into the DRS and the recyclables stream is
based on assumptions. It is assumed that 100% of brown and green glass, 40% of
clear glass, 30% of steel cans, 80% of aluminium cans and 60% of plastic bottles will
be returned via the DRS. Also, it is assumed that the materials are brought to the
Reverse Vending Machines (RVMs) by the citizens during their shopping and, thus,
the transport is not modelled. The electricity consumption of the RVM, as well as
its production, since it is a new capital good that will have to be produced for the
system, are based on data reported by Raadal et al. (2016) (Table 42, Appendix
C). The distance to the MRF was assumed to remain the same, since the process
is not expected to change, especially during the ﬁrst years where impurities could
still enter the system.
Thus, also the electricity and diesel consumption of the
facility remained equal to the MRF that handles the recyclable stream. However,
the sorting eﬃciencies, adjusted from Bisinella et al. (2018), are higher, at 95%
for all the materials apart from the plastic bottles, for which a 90% eﬃciency is
expected. The materials are recycled into new products using the same processes
and eﬃciencies as for the recyclable stream, while the rejects are sent to the ERF.
The only exception is the aluminium within the DRS, which is recycled with a
technological eﬃciency of 93%. The rest of the system is the same as in the Baseline
scenario. Details regarding the processes that were modelled on EASETECH can
be found in Appendix C, Table 41.
22
==PAGE:39==
Figure 7: Deposit Return Scheme scenario
23
==PAGE:40==
3.3.2.3
Separate Collection (SC) Scenario
In the third scenario, called Separate Collection (SC) scenario, the Baseline scenario
is altered by collecting the four fractions of recyclable materials separately, in ac-
cordance with article 28 of the Waste Framework Directive, which states that glass,
paper, plastic and metal should be collected separately if technically, environmen-
tally and economically practical (TEEP).
The kerbside collection of the recyclables from the Baseline scenario is split in
four processes, since it is assumed that each material will be collected separately in
a single collection vehicle. The distances required for the collection of the recyclable
streams are equal to the distance travelled for the recyclable waste collection in the
Baseline scenario. The separate collection results in less impurities in the streams
and, thus, it is assumed that a sorting process for each material is used before the
recycling on site. Glass is transported for 150 km to a recycling plant, where a
recycling process identical to the one in the Baseline scenario takes place. The other
materials, paper, metals and plastics, are transported to sorting facilities. The sort-
ing eﬃciencies of each facility can be found in Table 51, Appendix C.1. Paper is
recycled into new cardboard with a technological eﬃciency of 98% and a market
response of 90%, steel cans into new steel and aluminium cans into new aluminium,
following the same recycling process as the aluminium collected in the scheme in
the DRS scenario. Due to the large volume of plastics and the lack of facilities, it
is assumed that part of the collected material will be exported. Since the separate
collection is expected to result in higher quality of materials, 20% of the plastic bot-
tles and 20% of the soft plastic is expected to be accepted for recycling in Germany,
which is the most probable export destination in Europe (WRAP, 2015). Also, 45%
of the hard plastic, 10% of the plastic bottles and 50% of the soft plastic is expected
to be exported to Malaysia. The recycling processes modelled were the same for
the three countries. The rejects from the sorting facilities in the UK are sent to
the ERF. The only addition is the recycling process for HDPE, with a technological
eﬃciency of 90% and a market response of 81%. The rest of the system remains the
same as in the Baseline scenario. Details regarding the processes that were modelled
on EASETECH can be found in Appendix C, Table 43.

to recycling facilities in the UK and only 3% is exported. Also, 4% more waste is
sent to the ERF in comparison with the Baseline scenario.

3.4
Sensitivity Analysis
In order to provide robust results and support the conclusions and recommenda-
tions of the study, it is important to perform a sensitivity analysis (ILCD, 2010). In
this step, the sensitivity of the model, which is deﬁned by the extent to which an
assumption or a choice leads to variation of the obtained results, is tested. When a
model is sensitive toward a parameter, a small change in this parameter results in
large changes in the results (Hauschild et al., 2018).
The ﬁrst step for the analysis is the selection of the parameters that will be
tested, based on the process contribution analysis and the uncertainty of the assump-
tions made. The sensitivity analysis can be divided in two analyses, the perturbation
analysis and the scenario analysis. In the perturbation analysis, each tested parame-
ter is changed one at a time by a ﬁxed amount, e.g. 10%, while the other parameters
26
==PAGE:43==
remain ﬁxed. For each tested parameter, a new set of impact scores is calculated
and compared with the Baseline scenario. Eventually, the Sensitivity Ratio (SR),
which is the ratio between the relative change in the results of the impact categories
and the change in the parameters, is calculated as in Equation 1. A parameter is
deﬁned as sensitive if the absolute value of the average of the sensitivity ratios for all
the impact categories is higher than 0.3 or if the absolute maximum value is higher
than 0.5.
SR =
Result2−Result1
Result1
Parameter2−Parameter1
Parameter1
(1)
In addition, the SRs are normalised by dividing their absolute values with the ab-
solute maximum SR of each impact category using Equation 2. The most sensitive
parameter has normalised SR (nSR) equal to 1.
nSR =
| SR |
| MaxSR |
(2)
In the scenario analysis, system boundaries, technology eﬃciencies and other pa-
rameters can be changed at the same time, forming a possible alternative scenario
to the Baseline scenario. Then, for each tested scenario a new set of scores for each
impact category is calculated and compared with the initial results. To evaluate the
response of the systems, the relative changes are calculated using Equation 3. A
scenario is sensitive when the absolute maximum value obtained in at least one of
the impact categories is higher than 50%.
RelativeChange(%) = (Resultalternative −Resultinitial
Resultinitial
) ∗100%

3.5
LCC Methodology
In order to accurately estimate the cost of the waste management systems, the zero
burden approach was applied, which means that waste generation and upstream
activities, i.e. activities before the product becomes waste, are excluded. Since all
the management systems for the products, after they are characterised as waste,
will include these processes, and considering the comparative nature of the analysis,
the omission is justiﬁed (Hauschild et al., 2018). Additionally, the prices and costs
were not adjusted with inﬂation rates, since all the available data are recent and
discounting is not considered, which means that it is assumed that all the costs are
considered equally important, either they are happening in the present or in the
future.
This LCC takes the perspective of the local authorities and, thus, costs that
are incorporated in the gate fees of the facilities, such as the costs of management
of the residues from the processes, were not added in the system. The application
of the digestate and the compost on land is assumed to be equal to the application
27
==PAGE:44==
of conventional fertilisers, as Martinez-Sanchez et al. (2015) suggest, and, thus, it
was omitted from the calculations. Also, it was assumed that no capital goods, such
as collection bins and trucks, will need to be replaced during the year. An average
charge of £45 per bin per year was found for the collection of garden waste from
the local authorities and, in order to estimate the amount of bins charged, it was
assumed that 75% of the households are paying for the collection. The amount of
diesel required for the transport processes was calculated by multiplying the dis-
tances travelled with the amount of waste that needed to be transported and the
fuel consumption of the vehicle (see Table 58, Appendix D). The cost associated
with the collection vehicles includes their maintenance and insurance, as well as the
purchase cost of each vehicle is divided by 7 years, which is assumed to be their
lifetime, and is shown in Table 5. The collection crew consists of one driver and two
loaders for all the types of waste apart from the food waste. The number of vehicles
required for the collection was calculated by dividing the number of households with
the indicative pass rate and the frequency of collection. The indicative pass rate
represents the number of households each vehicle serves in one collection day. The
garden, recyclable and residual waste are collected fortnightly, while food waste is
collected weekly. The number of vehicles, drivers and loaders required to provide
the collection services can be seen in Table 6.
Table 5: Costs associated with the collection (Source: Private com-
pany)

For the DRS scenario, it was assumed that the RVM has a lifetime of 10 years
and only 30% of the total area of the county is considered as residential area, where
the machines are on average less than 5 km away from every household. Further-
more, it was assumed that, even though the scheme will be introduced, the collection
frequency will not change, since the response of the public to the new system is un-
certain. Thus, the collection costs for the DRS scenario are considered equal to the
costs of collection in the Baseline scenario, with the only diﬀerence appearing in the
fuel needed for the collection of the recyclables, which are expected to be less in
volume. Moreover, it is assumed that the scheme will not result in any revenue from
the deposit introduced on the containers, after the citizens returning the containers
will reclaim the deposit.
For the Separate Collection scenario, it was assumed that the pass rate of the
collection vehicles will be increased by 30% for the recyclables, since it will take more
bins to be emptied to completely ﬁll up the vehicle. Thus, the number of vehicles re-
quired for each fraction of the recyclables, and, consequently, the number of drivers
and loaders, is decreased by 30%, as seen in Table 7. The frequency is assumed to
remain the same. It is also assumed that, in order to provide the separate collec-
tion, new collection receptacles will be needed. For single-family households this
means that three additional boxes will be needed, while for multi-family households
a new large bin with multiple compartments is required, which is shared among ﬁve
multi-family households. Finally, it is assumed that the sorting facilities, where the
collected materials are sent to, charge the same gate fee as the other type of MRFs.

4
Results
The results were generated using the implemented impact assessment tool in EASE-
TECH. The Life Cycle Impact Assessment (LCIA) method used is called “ILCD
recommended 2013 Prosuit Global NR w/o LT” and contains impact categories rec-
ommended by the European Commission in the ILCD Handbook (EC-JRC, 2011)
with some alterations. First, for the impact category “depletion of abiotic resources”
the CML version is used instead of the oﬃcial ILCD method. In the CML version,
the impact category is split into “depletion of abiotic resources-fossil” and “de-
pletion of abiotic resources-elements”. Also, the selected LCIA method does not
include the “ionising radiation for ecosystem”, “land use” and “resource depletion-
water” impact categories. Additionally, it does not consider the “long term” com-
partment, meaning emissions that exceed 100 years (DTU Environment, 2018a).
Table 8 presents the impact categories that were considered, their units and the
abbreviations which will be used for the rest of this study.
The characterised results generated from the software are externally normalised
using the global normalisation references published in the Prosuite project (Laurent
et al., 2013). The normalisation factors can be found in Table 68, Appendix E.2.
These factors are used in order to obtain results with the same unit and to put
the results in a perspective, even though comparison across impact categories is not
possible, unless weighting is also applied. The values are given in Person Equivalent
(PE)*year, which corresponds to the impacts from one person in one year (Clavreul
et al., 2014).
The results are also internally normalised. With internal normalisation, the
results are divided by the absolute value of the total impact score of the Baseline
scenario for each impact category. The results are presented in percentages, with
the baseline scores equal to 100% for every impact category. It is worth mentioning
that, in LCA, negative values indicate environmental savings, while positive values
represent loads (Turconi et al., 2011).
30

Characterised & Normalised results
The characterised and the normalised results are reported in Tables 9 and 10, respec-

can be found in Appendix E.1, Tables 59-67, while the normalised results are shown

is the Separate Collection, which has the lowest impacts and the greatest beneﬁts
across all the impact categories, apart from the OD, where the Baseline scenario
performs better.

Figure 9: Externally normalised results
4.2
Process contribution
The contribution of aggregated processes to the absolute impact score is illustrated
in Figures 10, 11 and 12. Processes contributing less than 5% were omitted from
the graphs. The “Digestate UoL” process contains the use of the digestate on the
land and the substitution of the conventional fertilisers, while the “Compost UoL”
process contains the use of the compost on land and the substitution of conventional
fertilisers. The Energy from Waste (EfW) process contains the Ardley Energy Re-
covery Facility, the recycling processes for the IBA metals and the utilisation of
the ashes. The “Recycling within UK” process and the “Recycling in Malaysia”
process contain all the recycling processes taking place in the UK and in Malaysia,
respectively. Finally, the shipping process refers to the transport of the recyclables
to Malaysia. The shipping of the RDF to Sweden was included in a process called
RDF, which is not illustrated in the following graphs, due to its minor contribution.
The RDF process contains the transport, shipping and incineration in an ERF in
Sweden of the RDF, as well as the management of the residues of the ERF.
The most contributing process in each impact category for each scenario is pre-
sented in Tables 78 and 79 in Appendix E.3. Table 78 presents the process that
provides the highest negative impact, which is represented by positive values in the
scores, while Table 79 presents the process that provides the highest beneﬁt to the
system, which is represented by negative impact scores.
33
==PAGE:50==
Figure 10: Process contribution for the Baseline scenario
The processes that seem to be more impactful for the Baseline scenario are the
Compost UoL and the shipping processes, which are contributing positively to the
environmental impacts of the system, and the EfW and the Recycling within the
UK processes, which provide beneﬁts to the system.
The DRS process illustrated in Figure 11 contains the Reverse Vending Ma-
chine, the transport to MRF, the MRF and the recycling processes for the materials
collected through the scheme.
34
==PAGE:51==
Figure 11: Process contribution for the DRS scenario
For the DRS scenario, the shipping and the Compost UoL processes are con-
tributing in most of the impact categories with high percentages. Additionally, the
EfW and the DRS processes are providing environmental beneﬁts to the system.
However, it is important to mention that the DRS process contributes both posi-
tively and negatively to the impact categories.
35
==PAGE:52==
Figure 12: Process contribution for the Separate Collection scenario
The process contribution for the SC scenario shows a similar trend with the
Baseline scenario. However, since the amount of waste that is shipped is decreased
signiﬁcantly, the contribution of the shipping process to the impact scores is de-
creased.
4.3
Sensitivity Analysis results
4.3.1
Perturbation Analysis
The implemented tool in EASETECH was used for the perturbation analysis. The
default values were either increased or decreased by 10%, in order to avoid unreal-
istic values of eﬃciencies and ratios higher than 100%. All the parameters tested
in the Baseline scenario were also tested in the other two scenarios. However, due
to diﬀerent assumptions and processes in the DRS and the SC scenarios, additional
parameters were tested in these two scenarios. The detailed results of the perturba-
tion analysis can be found in Appendix F.1, Tables 80-85.
36
==PAGE:53==
4.3.1.1
Baseline Scenario
Table 11 presents the parameters that were tested in the perturbation analysis for
the Baseline scenario, together with their default values. Based on the process con-
tribution analysis that was conducted, the uncertainty of the assumptions made and
recommendations from the literature, it was considered important to test the sensi-
tivity of the parameters associated with the substitution of conventional fertilisers,
the substitution of virgin materials, the distance travelled to Malaysia, the sorting
eﬃciencies in the MRF, the eﬃciencies of the energy recovery systems, as well as
the emissions of nitrogen from the compost/digestate to the groundwater.

4.3.1.3
Separate Collection Scenario
For the separate collection scenario, some of the tested parameters had diﬀerent
values, since the sorting eﬃciencies in the single-stream MRFs were assumed to
be diﬀerent. Also, since a recycling process for HDPE was added in this scenario,
the substitution ratio for this material was tested too. The default values of these
parameters are presented in Table 17.

4.3.2
Scenario Analysis
The following scenarios were tested in all three scenarios:
• Heat Substitution: Heat recovery was added to the Ardley ERF process. Elec-
tricity eﬃciency drops to 22% from 27% and heat is recovered with 73% eﬃ-
ciency.
• Type of soil: Coarse sand soil was assumed to be representative instead of
heavy clay soil.
• Coal- UK: Coal was assumed to be the marginal electricity technology and,
thus, the electricity mix was replaced by coal in all the processes taking place
in the UK.
• Nuclear: Nuclear technology was assumed to be the marginal electricity tech-
nology and, thus, it replaced the modelled electricity mix in all the processes
taking place in the UK.
• Coal- MY: Coal was assumed to be the marginal electricity technology in
Malaysia and, thus, it replaced the modelled electricity mix in all the processes
taking place in Malaysia.
The results of these alternative scenarios compared with the initial scenarios
are presented in Figure 13. Climate change was selected as the impact category
that inﬂuences most the political decisions and it was one of the categories that
were sensitive to changes.
43
==PAGE:60==
Figure 13: Comparison of the initial results with the common alternative scenarios tested
in the Scenario Analysis
4.3.2.1
Baseline Scenario
In addition to the scenarios described above, the following scenarios were also tested:
• Landﬁlling of ashes: The ﬂy and bottom ashes from the Ardley ERF were sent
to the landﬁll instead of being utilised. This scenario was not tested in the
DRS and the SC scenario because of the negligible relative change.
• 50% export: 50% of all the recyclable materials, apart from glass, steel and
aluminium, is exported.
The results showed that the Baseline scenario is sensitive to all the alternative
scenarios tested, except for the scenarios where the digestate and the compost are
applied on a diﬀerent type of soil and where the ashes from the ERF were landﬁlled.
The detailed results can be seen in Table 86, Appendix F.2.
4.3.2.2
DRS Scenario
In addition to the scenarios that were mentioned in 4.3.2, the following scenarios
were also tested:
44
==PAGE:61==
• 50% export: 50% of all the recyclable materials, apart from glass, steel and
aluminium and the ones entering the DRS, is exported.
• With car: A transport of an average distance of 5 km by car was added in the
scenario, representing the transport of the recyclables from the households to
the RVMs.
• Rejects landﬁlled: The rejects from the MRF accepting the recyclables of the
DRS are landﬁlled instead of being incinerated.
The results from the scenario analysis for the DRS scenario showed that the scenario
is sensitive in all the alternatives tested, apart from the scenario with the diﬀerent
type of soil and the scenario with the landﬁlling of the MRF rejects. The detailed
results can be seen in Table 87, Appendix F.2.
4.3.2.3
Separate Collection Scenario
In addition to the scenarios that were mentioned in 4.3.2, the following scenarios
were also tested:
• No export: All plastics are recycled in the UK and, thus, no materials are
exported to Germany or to Malaysia.
• Coal- DE: Coal was assumed to be the marginal electricity technology in Ger-
many and, thus, the modelled electricity mix was replaced by coal in the
recycling of the plastics.
This scenario was found to be sensitive only to a change in the technology used
for electricity produced in the UK. The detailed results can be seen in Table 88,
Appendix F.2.
The results of the two alternative scenarios that were found to be sensitive in all
three scenarios are illustrated in Figures 14 and 15. As in the other graphs, the
AD-F and AD-E categories are not illustrated due to the much higher scores.
45

Figure 15: Results of the Nuclear alternative scenario for the three scenarios
