5
Discussion
Figure 16: Internally normalised results
The impact scores for HTc, HTnc, IR, EuM and EcoF do not vary more than 20%
across the three scenarios. However, for the CC impact category, the SC scenario
has two times lower impact score than the Baseline scenario. For the TA and POF
impact categories, the SC scenario is environmentally beneﬁcial, contrary to the
other two scenarios which have positive impact scores. In EuT, although all the
scenarios have positive impact scores, the SC has the lowest, which is 24% of the
impact score of the Baseline scenario. It is worth mentioning that three impact
categories, the PM, AD-F and AD-E, are not illustrated in Figure 16, because the
diﬀerences across the scenarios were very high. For the PM, the SC scenario has
a negative impact score, which is almost seven times the positive impact score of
the Baseline scenario for this category. In addition, the SC scenario has an impact
score seventeen times lower than the score of the Baseline scenario for the AD-F.
The largest diﬀerence observed is found on the impact scores of the AD-E impact
category, where the SC and DRS scenarios have a signiﬁcantly lower impact score
than the Baseline scenario.
5.1
Process contribution
The processes modelled for the collection, the AD, the IVC, the MRF, the landﬁlling,
the transport and the RDF contributed less than 5% each in each impact category
in all three scenarios. The only exception is the landﬁll in the SC scenario, which
53
==PAGE:70==
contributed 6% in the OD impact category. Thus, these processes were considered
not important for the modelled systems and no sensitivity analysis was performed
in order to test the uncertainty of the assumptions made for them.
Below, the
most contributing processes in both the positive and the negative impact scores are
presented for each impact category. The percentages can be found in Tables 78 and
79, Appendix E.3.
5.1.1
Climate Change (CC)
The process that contributes most with positive impact scores to this impact cat-
egory is the Ardley ERF. This impact can be attributed to the fact that 45% and
47% of the waste in the Baseline, the DRS and the SC scenario are incinerated
respectively. The process that contributes most with negative impact scores is the
recycling of paper in the UK in the Baseline and the DRS scenarios and the re-
cycling of paper and card in the SC scenario. This fact can be attributed to the
large amount of paper in the recyclables (20%), as well as to the substitution of
virgin paper in the two scenarios and the substitution of virgin cardboard in the SC
scenario.
5.1.2
Ozone Depletion (OD)
The landﬁll seems to be the process that contributes most in this impact category.
The positive impact scores across all three scenarios in this category are dominated
by this process, due to the release of harmful chemicals from the waste. However,
the beneﬁts of the modelled systems are much higher than the environmental loads
for this category.
In the aseline scenario, this can be attributed to recycling of
aluminium and the consequent substitution of virgin aluminium. In the other two
scenarios, the negative impacts are the results of the recycling of the aluminium
retrieved from the IBA of the Ardley ERF.
5.1.3
Human Toxicity (HT)
For both cancer and non-cancer eﬀects, the positive impact scores can be attributed
to the application of the compost on the soil, which requires diesel-powered ma-
chines. However, the environmental gains for this category stem from the substi-
tution of conventional fertilisers from the digestate for the cancer eﬀects and the
substitution of conventional fertilisers from the compost for the non- cancer eﬀects.
The production of mineral fertilisers requires a mix of chemicals, which are not
necessary when digestate or compost is used.
54
==PAGE:71==
5.1.4
Particulate matter (PM)
The shipping of recyclable paper to Malaysia is the most contributing process to the
PM impact category for the Baseline and the DRS scenarios. For the SC scenario,
the shipping of the recyclable plastic to Malaysia is the most contributing process.
This process is harmful due to the large distance travelled by ship, which is powered
with fossil fuels.
For the Baseline scenario, the most beneﬁcial process for this
category is the recycling of aluminium and the consequent substitution of primary
aluminium, while for the other two scenarios it is the Ardley ERF and the avoided
production of electricity with the national energy mix.
5.1.5
Ionising Radiation (IR)
For this impact category, the most impactful process was the recycling of paper in
the UK for the Baseline and the DRS scenario and the recycling of glass for the SC
scenario. In all three cases, the use of electricity produced with the electricity mix
modelled is to be blamed. However, since electricity produced in the Ardley ERF
substitutes electricity produced from other sources, the environmental gains in all
three scenarios are higher than the loads.
5.1.6
Photochemical Ozone Formation (POF)
The use of container ships powered with fossil fuels is the process that contributes
most with positive impact scores for the three scenarios. In the Baseline scenario,
the process that contributes most with negative impact scores is the recycling of
aluminium, which substitutes the production of primary aluminium. In the DRS
scenario, the recycling of PET collected through the scheme contributes most to the
negative impact scores, due to the substitution of virgin PET. In the SC scenario,
the recycling of paper and card contributes most to the negative impact scores, due
to the substitution of virgin cardboard.
5.1.7
Terrestrial Acidiﬁcation (TA)
In the Baseline and the DRS scenarios, the process that contributes most to the
positive impacts in this category is the shipping of paper to Malaysia. In the SC
scenario, the most impactful process is the recycling of aluminium, due to the needs
of the recycling process. The most environmentally beneﬁcial process to this cate-
gory is the recycling of aluminium in the Baseline scenario, the Ardley ERF in the
DRS scenario, due to the substitution of electricity, and the recycling of paper and
card in the SC scenario, due to the substitution of virgin cardboard.
55
==PAGE:72==
5.1.8
Terrestrial Eutrophication (EuT)
In the Baseline and the DRS scenarios, the shipping process of the paper to Malaysia
contributes most to the positive impact scores in this category. In the SC scenario,
the most impactful process is the application of the digestate on land, due to the
leaching of the nutrients. The process that contributes most to the negative impact
scores is the recycling of paper in the UK for the Baseline scenario, the recycling
of aluminium for the DRS scenario and the recycling of paper and card for the SC
scenario, due to the substitution of virgin materials.
5.1.9
Freshwater Eutrophication (EuF)
In this impact category, the process that contributes most is the application of
compost on the land for the Baseline and the SC scenario and the Reverse Vending
Machine process for the DRS scenario. The contribution of the compost can be
attributed to the leaching of nutrients, while the contribution of the RVM can be
attributed to the materials needed for its production. In all three scenarios, the most
environmentally beneﬁcial process is the substitution of conventional fertilisers from
the digestate, due to the high content of the digestate in phosphorus.
5.1.10
Marine Eutrophication (EuM)
For this impact category, the most harmful process is the application of digestate
on land, because of the leaching of the nutrients. In the Baseline and the DRS
scenarios, the most beneﬁcial process is the recycling of cardboard in Malaysia, due
to the substitution of cardboard, while in the SC scenario the most beneﬁcial process
is the substitution of conventional fertilisers from the compost.
5.1.11
Freshwater ecotoxicity (EcoF)
In this impact category, for all three scenarios the most harmful process is the
application of the compost on land, due to the use of diesel-powered machinery.
The most beneﬁcial process is the Ardley ERF, due to the avoided production of
electricity.
5.1.12
Depletion of abiotic resources (fossil) (AD-F)
The most impactful process in the Baseline and the DRS scenarios is the recycling
of cardboard in Malaysia, because of the electricity required for the process. In the
SC scenario, the most impactful process is the recycling of glass, also because of the
56
==PAGE:73==
energy needed. However, the substitution of electricity produced in the Ardley ERF
contributes most in the negative impact scores in the Baseline scenario, cancelling
out the positive impact score of the recycling of cardboard in Malaysia. In the DRS
and the SC scenarios, the recycling of aluminium contributes almost 100% to the
environmental beneﬁts of the systems in this impact category.
5.1.13
Depletion of abiotic resources (elements) (AD-E)
The recycling of paper in the UK in the Baseline scenario is its most impactful
process, due to the electricity needed for the process. In the DRS scenario, the
RVM contributes most, due to the materials needed for its production. In the SC
scenario, the sorting facility for paper contributes most, because of the need for
electricity. The most beneﬁcial process across all the scenarios is the recycling of
aluminium. Its contribution to the negative impact scores is almost equal to 100%
for the DRS and the SC scenarios, because a diﬀerent recycling process was used,
considering the higher quality of the input in these two scenarios.
5.2
Sensitivity Analysis
In all three scenarios, the parameters expressing the sorting eﬃciency for paper,
the substitution ratio for aluminium, card and glass, the ratio of recovered electric-
ity in the ERF and the substitution ratio for the fertiliser containing phosphorus
were found to be sensitive. In the Baseline scenario, the most sensitive parameters
were the sorting eﬃciency and the substitution ratio for aluminium, the shipping
distance and the ratio of recovered electricity. A change of 10% in the ratio of re-
covered electricity would result in, at least, two times higher or lower impact scores
in ﬁve impact categories. The same eﬀect would occur by changing the shipping
distance, which aﬀects four impact categories. In the DRS scenario, the most sen-
sitive parameters were found to be the shipping distance and the substitution ratio
for fertiliser containing phosphorus. A change of 10% in these parameters would
result in an increase in the PM impact score by 380% and in the EuF impact score
by 292%. However, the substitution ratio for the fertiliser would only aﬀect the
freshwater eutrophication substantially, while the shipping distance aﬀects four im-
pact categories. It is, also, worth mentioning that, even though the sensitivity ratio
for the recovered electricity was not as high as for the aforementioned parameters,
this parameter would aﬀect four impact categories if it was to be changed by 10%.
Finally, in the SC scenario, the most sensitive parameters are the sorting eﬃciency
for aluminium, the substitution ratio for aluminium, card and glass and the ratio of
recovered electricity from the ERF. A change in all of these parameters would result
in higher impact scores for the TA category, except from the sorting eﬃciency, which
would result in lower impact scores. The contribution of the recovered electricity
ratio in the impact categories is signiﬁcant, aﬀecting half of them.
57
==PAGE:74==
While testing the diﬀerent scenarios that could result in a change of at least
50% in the impact scores, it was found that the results of the three scenarios would
change if only coal or nuclear power were used to produce the electricity consumed
in the processes occurring in the UK. These two alternative scenarios showed the
highest sensitivity among the tested scenarios, underlining the importance of the
electricity mix in the model.
However, as Figures 14 and 15 show, the changes
would not change the ranking of the scenarios. The results of the Baseline scenario
would also change if heat was substituted in the ERF too, resulting in reduction of
the CC impact score by 67%. Additionally, coal-generated electricity in Malaysia
would result in an increase of 259% in the PM impact score and 50% export of the
recyclables would lead in a decrease in the PM impact score by 237%. The same
trend is found for the results of the DRS scenario. However, the changes in the
impact scores would be smaller. Moreover, it was found that, if the householders
were using their cars to transfer the recyclables to the RVMs, then the impact score
for the CC would be higher by 59%. It is worth mentioning that the alternative
scenario where only coal was used to produce the electricity in Malaysia has an eﬀect
on the two scenarios because of the recycling of the cardboard which contributes to
the PM impact category. The SC scenario was not sensitive to the other alternative
scenarios tested, apart from the ones where the electricity technology in the UK was
changed.
5.3
LCC
As illustrated in Figure 17, the current waste management system in Oxfordshire
has the lowest cost among the three scenarios.
Almost half of the cost for the
Baseline scenario, 45%, consists of the gate fee required for the waste to be accepted
in the Ardley ERF. The second highest cost is associated with the collection of
the waste, covering 33% of the total cost.
The DRS scenario costs almost 1.1
million pounds more than the Baseline scenario, due to the introduction of the
scheme in the system and the need to buy RVMs, which account for 78% of this
diﬀerence in the costs between the two scenarios. The SC scenario appears to be the
most expensive, with a diﬀerence of 8.6 million pounds from the Baseline scenario.
This diﬀerence can be attributed mostly to the high collection costs required for
the separate collection of the materials.
The revenues from the products of the
processes and the recyclables are almost equal across the three scenarios, except
from the revenue of the recyclables in the SC scenario, where the value is higher
by £834,560. The materials that provided most of the revenues in the scenarios
were the electricity which was recovered in the ERF and the aluminium. It is, also,
worth mentioning that, in the SC scenario, 34% of the revenues from the recyclable
materials comes from HDPE, which was found to be a material of high value.
58
==PAGE:75==
Figure 17: Comparison of cost and revenues for the three scenarios
In Figure 18, it can be seen that the scenario with the biggest diﬀerence between
the cost and the revenues is the SC scenario. This diﬀerence is twice as high as the
diﬀerence in the other two scenarios. However, this diﬀerence could be decreased
by cutting the collection costs and by achieving higher quality of the recyclable
materials with less impurities.
Figure 18: Revenues and cost for each scenario
59
==PAGE:76==
As stated by DEFRA (2018d), the government is willing to provide the local
authorities with additional resources in order to cover costs arising from newly imple-
mented policies. Thus, the implementation of a separate collection could be feasible,
if the local authorities made sure that it would be environmentally beneﬁcial. Fur-
thermore, the collection cost could change signiﬁcantly, if EPR is applied, making
the producers responsible for collecting the waste associated with their products or
fund the management of packaging at the end of its life.
5.4
Interpretation
The scenario that was found to perform better than the other two was the scenario
where paper, glass, metals and plastics were collected separately.
The Baseline
scenario performed better only in the OD impact category, due to the diﬀerent recy-
cling process for aluminium used in this scenario. The results showed that the DRS
scenario performs similarly or, in some impact categories, worse than the Baseline
scenario.
This can be, partly, attributed to the fact that the DRS scenario in-
cludes the production of the RVMs needed for the scheme, which is a process that
contributes with positive scores to the total impacts. However, it is considered im-
portant to include this process in the scenario, because the implementation of the
scheme would result in the production of additional RVMs. The application of either
the compost or the digestate on the land, as well as the shipping of recyclables to
Malaysia, were the processes that were found to contribute most to the three scenar-
ios. The most beneﬁcial processes were the substitution of conventional fertilisers,
the Ardley ERF and the recycling processes for aluminium, paper and card.
The perturbation analysis showed that the substitution ratios for aluminium,
card and glass are sensitive parameters in the three scenarios, which is accordance
with the ﬁndings of Bassi et al. (2017), who identiﬁes these materials together with
paper as the most inﬂuencing of the management systems. Also, Laurent et al.
(2014b) mention the importance of the substitution ratios in the ﬁnal LCA results,
since an overestimation can aﬀect signiﬁcantly the beneﬁts resulting from the recy-
cling processes. Additionally, the shipping distance was found to be an important
parameter, which is reasonable considering the fact that the process was found to
be the most contributing in many impact categories. An inﬂuential parameter in all
three scenarios was also the ratio of recovered electricity in the ERF.
The scenario analysis showed the sensitivity of the three scenarios to the tech-
nology used to produce the electricity needed in the processes, which is a common
issue in LCAs (Finnveden et al., 2005).
The sensitivity analysis showed that the results of the modelled systems vary,
according to speciﬁc choices and assumptions. However, the variance is not enough
to change the ranking of the scenarios. The LCC showed that the SC scenario would
be the most expensive to implement. However, the high cost associated with the
collection costs could be decreased by using multi-compartment vehicles that have
lower running and capital costs and require less employees and by implementing the
EPR policy. Also, higher quality of recyclables could lead to higher revenues.
60
==PAGE:77==
The outcome of the scenarios indicates the importance of waste being handled
domestically, instead of being shipped to other countries. This approach on the
export of waste would decrease the dependence on other countries, especially in
Southeast Asia, that bear the weight of safely treating and disposing large amounts
of waste, frequently of low quality. Since China has stopped accepting low quality
recyclables, and Philippines and Malaysia are now sending back containers that do
not get accepted anymore, the need for higher quality recycling in closer proximity
emerges. Moreover, domestic recycling would result in increased control over the
eﬃciency of the recycling process and would ensure that the materials are not mis-
managed.
With regard to the anticipated BREXIT, DEFRA (2018d), in the Waste &
Resources Strategy, states that the environmental laws and policies imposed by the
EU will be retained in the UK law. Furthermore, it is stated that the country will
have the opportunity to adapt the policies to the local needs and conditions. The
EU targets will be reviewed, in order to aim at higher recycling rates, if possible,
and the environmental impacts of waste will be considered, contributing to environ-
mental improvement and growth that the country wishes to achieve. Thus, it is not
expected that the BREXIT will aﬀect negatively the waste management system.
However, the exports of waste and other materials could be more diﬃcult to handle,
leading to the need for more domestic reprocessors and facilities. Eﬀective treat-
ment of both recyclable and residual waste would be necessary, since waste that are
exported to incineration facilities would also be aﬀected.
61
==PAGE:78==
6
Conclusions
The management of waste generated in households in the area of Oxfordshire was
assessed from an environmental and ﬁnancial perspective. It was possible to acquire
area-speciﬁc data regarding the waste composition and its management. However,
data regarding processes in the UK were scarce. The two alternative scenarios were
modelled based on current trends in policies and environmental targets in the UK.
The study conducted resulted in the following conclusions:
• The SC scenario is the most preferable system for all the impact categories,
apart from the Ozone Depletion.
• For the Baseline scenario, the processes that contribute most with positive
scores are the application of compost and digestate on land and the shipping
of recyclables to Malaysia.
• The most beneﬁcial, from environmental point of view, process in the Baseline
scenario is the recycling of aluminium.
• The electricity used for the processes is a crucial parameter that inﬂuences
signiﬁcantly the scenarios.
• The substitution ratio for aluminium is a sensitive parameter.
• The current waste management system, modelled as the Baseline scenario, is
the least expensive.
• The collection and the gate fee for the ERF are the most expensive parts of
the system.
• The electricity that is recovered in the ERF, as well as the recycling of alu-
minium, provide the highest revenues.
• The SC scenario would be the most expensive to implement.
Taking the above results into consideration, the SC scenario is the most preferable
system. The proximity of the facilities constitutes an important parameter to the
reduction of the environmental impact of this system in comparison with the other
two that were modelled. Although this scenario was found to be the most expen-
sive, due to high collection costs, the implementation of the Extended Producer
Responsibility could reduce the cost. In addition, separate collection would result
in less impurities and recyclables of better quality, which could increase their prices.
Finally, the TEEP term includes the technical part, which requires that the system
has been implemented in an authority with similar relevant characteristics and is
proven to function. Furthermore, the capacity of existing processing facilities should
be ensured, so that the collected materials can be recycled in domestic sites.

==PAGE:85==
Appendices
A
Goal Deﬁnition
A.1
Intended Applications
The objective of this LCA study is the comparison between the current household
municipal waste management system applied in Oxfordshire and improved scenarios.
Additionally, the study will identify the weak environmental points of the currently
applied system (hotspot analysis). The ﬁnancial costs and beneﬁts of the analysed
systems will be identiﬁed in a cLCC, in order to verify the results of the LCA.
The outcome is expected to be used from the Oxfordshire County Council as guid-
ance to improve the carbon footprint of the county and optimise the current waste
management system in terms of environmental performance.
A.2
Method Assumptions and Impact Limitations
The study is limited by the area where it is applied. The project only considers the
household waste produced in Oxfordshire, thus disregarding waste imported from
other counties to be treated in facilities located in Oxfordshire and waste generated
from other sectors. Also, waste that is not collected from the local authorities, such
as bulky waste and waste that residents dispose at bring sites or civic amenity sites
are disregarded. In addition, the recommended impact methods do not allow for
spatially speciﬁc impact assessment. Thus, the impact scores are calculated as if
the potential impacts arose in any part of Europe.
A.3
Reasons for Carrying out the LCA Study and Decision Context
The City Council has not conducted a LCA to assess the environmental impacts
that result from managing the household waste collected in the area. Since envi-
ronmental awareness is becoming a prerequisite, both legally and socially, this need
has now emerged.
This study is expected to assist the decision making and future planning regard-
ing the environmentally preferred management of the household waste conducted by
the District Council. Consequently, the decision context is identiﬁed as “A: Micro-
level decision support”, which means that any decisions taken will not lead to any
structural changes to the background system. The changes that will possibly derive
from this study are only expected to aﬀect the background system in a limited way.
Any change regarding recyclable materials substituting for virgin materials may only
aﬀect the background system in a small percentage, which considering the national
69
==PAGE:86==
or global substitution is negligible. Likewise, additional production of energy from
the waste, either with incineration or anaerobic digestion, or reduction in energy use
will not signiﬁcantly change the national electricity or energy mix and use.
A.4
Target Audience
The target audience of this study consists of the supervisor, Dr. Anders Damgaard,
and external reviewers with knowledge on LCA. Also, this study will be used from
the Oxfordshire District Council members, who have limited knowledge on the LCA
methodology.
A.5
Comparative Assertions to be Disclosed to the Public
This comparative study will not be disclosed to the public.
A.6
Commissioner of the LCA Study and Other Inﬂuential Actors
The commissioner of the study is the Technical University of Denmark (DTU).
Parties expected to inﬂuence the outcome include the Oxfordshire Distict Council
members.
70
==PAGE:87==
B
Scope Deﬁnition
B.1
Deliverables
The deliverables of this study consist of: (i) detailed model of the life cycle of the
collected household waste in the county of Oxfordshire, (ii) detailed models with
suggested improvements for the solid waste management system regarding house-
hold waste, (iii) LCIA results with interpretation, (iv) ﬁnancial LCC for the three
scenarios.
B.2
Function, Functional Unit and Reference Flow
In order to allow future comparisons and increase the transparency of the study,
the function, the Functional Unit and the reference ﬂow of the system have to be
deﬁned.
The function of the presented system is to collect, transport, treat and dispose
the solid waste generated in households in the county of Oxfordshire and collected
from the local authorities. The system must suﬃciently remove the waste from the
residents’ houses and manage it so it is not a burden to the citizens anymore. In
addition, the waste management must comply with the English and European legis-
lation, as deﬁned by the Waste (England and Wales) Regulations 2011 (SI 2011/127,
2011) and the Waste Framework Directive (European Commission, 2008). Some po-
sitioning properties that could make one system preferable over the other are the
cost, the environmental impacts, the amount of materials and energy that the sys-
tem can recover, the compliance with directives and strategies, the convenience it
oﬀers to the citizens and the safety it can provide to the workers.
The functional unit is deﬁned to increase the transparency of this study and
support future comparisons (Hauschild et al., 2018). In this LCA, the functional
unit is deﬁned as the management of the household waste generated in the county
of Oxfordshire and collected from the local authorities during the 2017/18 ﬁnan-
cial year (01/04/2017-31/03/2018), in accordance with the regulation, as deﬁned
above.
The amount corresponding to this statement (reference ﬂow) is 258,725
tonnes (WasteDataFlow, 2019). The waste composition, which derived from data
on WasteDataFlow (2019) and conﬁdential data, is shown in Table 30.

B.3
LCI Modelling and Handling of Multifunctional Processes
The management of the waste results in multiple outputs, since modern systems
not only dispose the waste, but they are designed to achieve the highest rate of
recovery of materials and energy. For example, incineration does not only reduce
the volume of the waste, but also provides energy in the form of electricity and
heat. Also, recycling leads to the substitution of virgin materials while avoiding the
disposal of the used materials. Anaerobic Digestion produces electricity and heat,
taking advantage of the properties of organic materials.
Even landﬁlling, which
nowadays is the least preferable option (European Commission, 2008), results in the
production of methane that is used for electricity production. Thus, it is necessary
to model these multifunctional processes in the most representative way for the
systems, in order to link one speciﬁc process with the corresponding environmental
impact, practically isolating them from the other systems they are interacting with.
According with the hierarchy recommended in the ISO 14044 standard (ISO, 2006)
and the ILCD guidelines (ILCD, 2010), the preferable LCI modelling framework for
decision context A is the attributional with system expansion. In this framework,
average processes are used to model the background system.
B.4
System Boundaries and Completeness Requirements
The system boundaries deﬁne the boundaries between the waste management sys-
tem and the surrounding economy (technosphere) and the environment (ecosphere)
72
==PAGE:89==
(Hauschild et al., 2018).
The system boundaries of this study are illustrated in
Figure 19. The dashed line shows the boundary between the background and the
foreground system. The boxes in the background system show the inputs that are
required from the system and the outputs that result from it, while the arrows indi-
cate the waste ﬂows. The dotted arrows show the avoided production of materials
or energy in the background system. The household waste generated are collected
and transported in three diﬀerent fractions, the organics, the residuals and the recy-
clables. The organic fraction is transported to a biological treatment facility, which
may be either an AD plant or a composting plant. Its treatment results in the pro-
duction of electricity and heat and the formation of either digestate or compost. The
digestate or the compost are credited for substituting conventional fertilisers, while
the electricity and the heat are credited for the corresponding amount the process
provides. The recyclables are transported to a MRF, where a certain amount gets
recycled and, thus, substitutes virgin materials, while the rest, which does not fulﬁl
the requirements for recycling, is transported to a facility, which may be a landﬁll, a
RDF facility or an incineration plant. The RDF, after its formation, is incinerated
in the ERF plant. Finally, the residuals are transported to an incineration plant or
a landﬁll, where the incineration produces electricity, heat, bottom ash and ﬂy ash
and the landﬁll produces electricity. The bottom ash substitutes primary or sec-
ondary aggregates, the electricity and heat substitute a certain amount in the grid
and the ﬂy ash is landﬁlled. Transport is included in this study, since in the case
of waste LCAs, it can contribute signiﬁcantly to the environmental impacts (Clift
et al., 2000). Also, the capital goods are included in the system boundaries, since,
as Brogaard and Christensen (2016) concluded, they contribute signiﬁcantly in the
environmental impact of waste management systems. However, in this study exist-
ing infrastructure is used and, thus, capital goods are not expected to contribute
signiﬁcantly (more than 5%).
Initially, no processes are excluded from the system boundaries, since the goal
includes also the identiﬁcation of the environmental impacts of the current solid
waste management system. However, in the comparison between diﬀerent scenar-
ios, omission of identical processes is acceptable, since their environmental impacts
will be equal.
73
==PAGE:90==
Figure 19: System boundaries

==PAGE:91==
B.5
Representativeness of LCI Data
B.5.1
Geographical Representativeness
This study is speciﬁc for Oxfordshire. Data regarding waste generation and man-
agement are expected to be found for the county. However, in case data are not
available speciﬁcally for this county, data from England are still considered repre-
sentative, since the national policy and legislation and the cultural background do
not diﬀer. The technologies modelled for the treatment of waste are modelled with
data from the facilities where household waste are directed, although other processes
from Northern Europe could also be representative, due to similar technology used
and climate conditions. Furthermore, the electricity used for the processes of the
system is modelled speciﬁcally for the UK, since the exact mix of electricity sources
within the county could not be identiﬁed.
B.5.2
Time-related Representativeness
Since the waste management sector and the technologies associated with it are evolv-
ing rapidly, the time frame in which this study and the data used are representative
has to be deﬁned. This study is expected to be representative for ﬁve years, Due to
behavioural changes and the rise of environmental awareness, the waste generation
is continually changing. Additionally, new policies and laws are being issued by the
government, making changes in the amount and the composition of the waste and
the technologies used possible. Also, the development of the technology can aﬀect
signiﬁcantly the waste management, since new materials may be released and new
treatment options could be introduced.
B.5.3
Technological Representativeness
The technology used to manage the household waste should be representative of Ox-
fordshire or, whenever this is not possible, of England. Processes used in Northern
Europe could, also, be representative, since the technology and the climate condi-
tions are quite similar.
B.6
Basis for Impact Assessment
Taking into consideration the goal of this study, all the environmental impacts avail-
able are considered, in order to avoid shifting the environmental burden from one
Area of Protection to another. Consequently, the impact categories that are included
in the study are: climate change (kg CO2-eq), ozone depletion (kg CFC-11-eq), hu-
man toxicity (cancer and non-cancer eﬀects, CTUh), particulate matter (PM2.5-eq),
75
==PAGE:92==
ionising radiation (kBq U235-eq), photochemical ozone formation (kg NMVOC), ter-
restrial acidiﬁcation (mol H+-eq), terrestrial eutrophication (mol N-eq), freshwater
eutrophication (kg P-eq), marine eutrophication (kg N-eq), freshwater ecotoxicity
(CTUe), depletion of fossil abiotic resources (MJ) and depletion of elements (kg
antimony-eq), as recommended by EC-JRC (2011).
The solid waste management systems are modelled using the EASETECH
model, in which the “ILCD recommended 2013 Prosuit Global NR w/o LT” is
chosen for the LCIA. This impact method includes the normalisation reference from
Laurent et al. (2013) and does not take into account the emissions that will occur
in more than 100 years.
B.7
Requirements for Comparative Studies
Requirements for comparative studies should be deﬁned in case this study was to
be disclosed to the public. Since this study is expected to be used internally by the
commissioners, special requirements are not necessarily fulﬁlled.
B.8
Critical Review Needs
A critical review is not needed, since the study will not be disclosed to the public.

C.2
Data quality assessment
The methodology used to assess the quality of the data used in this study was
developed by Weidema and Wesnaes (1996). Each data input is assigned a value from
1 to 5, where 1 represents a very good quality level and 5 a very poor quality level.
The indicators are presented in Table 52, as described by ILCD (2010). The speciﬁc
quality corresponding to each indicator is shown in Figure 20, where reliability refers
to precision.
Table 52: Data quality indicators (ILCD, 2010)
Technological
representa-
tiveness
Degree to which the data set reﬂects the true population of in-
terest regarding technology, including for included background
data sets, if any
Geographical
representa-
tiveness
Degree to which the data set reﬂects the true population of in-
terest regarding geography, including for included background
data sets, if any
Temporal
representa-
tiveness
Degree to which the data set reﬂects the true population of in-
terest regarding time / age of the data, including for included
background data sets, if any
Completeness
Share of (elementary) ﬂows that are quantitatively included in
the inventory. Note that for product and waste ﬂows this needs
to be judged on a system’s level
Precision
Measure of the variability of the data values for each data ex-
pressed (e.g. low variance = high precision)
95
==PAGE:112==
Figure 20: Pedigree matrix with ﬁve data quality indicators (Weidema and Wesnaes,
1996)
After assigning the scores to the data, another indicator suggested by ILCD
(2010) is calculated in order to evaluate the overall data quality of the data set.
The Data Quality Rating (DQR) is calculated based on the following formula:
DQR = TeR + GR + TiR + C + P + M + Xw ∗4
i + 4
(4)
where TeR: Technological Representantiveness, GR: Geographical Representative-
ness, TiR: Time-Related Representativeness, C: Completeness, P: precision, M:
Methodological Appropriateness and consistency, Xw: weakest score, i: number of
scores. Methodological appropriateness and consistency, which refers to the degree
to which the applied methods and methodological choices of the data used are in
line with the goal and scope of the study, was not included in the calculations, due
to lack of documentation for most of the data used regarding this indicator.
Based on ILCD (2010), when the DQR is lower than 1.6, the overall data qual-
ity level is high, between 1.6 and 3 the quality is basic, while when it is higher than
3 the quality is considered a data estimation.
The data quality is shown in Tables 53, 54 and 55. Data retrieved from Waste-
DataFlow are considered of high quality, while data from literature are of basic
quality. Data from processes used on EASETECH have the lowest quality, mainly
because of the low scores in technological and temporal representativeness.

==PAGE:183==
Glossary
Term
Deﬁnition
Reference
Area of Protection
Endpoint categories with recognisable
value to the society: Human Health,
Natural Resources, Natural Environ-
ment
Hauschild
et al. (2018)
Attributional modelling
Modelling framework that inventories
the inputs and output ﬂows of all pro-
cesses of a system as they occur
ILCD (2010)
Backﬁlling
A recovery operation where waste is
used in excavated areas (such as un-
derground mines, gravel pits) for the
purpose of slope reclamation or safety
or for engineering purposes in landscap-
ing and where the waste is substituting
other non-waste materials which would
have had to be used for the purpose
DEFRA
(2019)
Background system
The processes of a system that are not
speciﬁc to it
Hauschild
et al. (2018)
Biodegradable waste
Any waste that is capable of undergo-
ing anaerobic or aerobic decomposition,
such as food and garden waste, paper
and cardboard
European
Commission
(1999)
Capital goods
Machinery, infrastructure, equipment
Hauschild
et al. (2018)
Characterisation
A step of the impact assessment, in
which the environmental interventions
assigned qualitatively to a particular
impact category (in classiﬁcation) are
quantiﬁed in terms of a common unit
for that category, allowing aggregation
into one ﬁgure of the indicator result
Hauschild
et al. (2018)
Classiﬁcation
A step of impact assessment, in which
environmental
interventions
are
as-
signed to predeﬁned impact categories
on a purely qualitative basis
Hauschild
et al. (2018)
Collection
The gathering of waste, including the
preliminary sorting and preliminary
storage of waste for the purposes of
transport to a waste treatment facility
European
Commission
(2008)
167
==PAGE:184==
Critical review
Process intended to ensure consistency
between a life cycle assessment and the
principles and requirements of the In-
ternational Standards on life cycle as-
sessment
ISO (2006)
Data quality
Characteristics of data that relate to
their ability to satisfy stated require-
ments
ISO (2006)
Decision context A
The results will be used to support
a decision, but the small scale of the
studied product system means that re-
gardless the decision made, it will not
cause structural changes in the systems
that the studied system interacts with.
Hauschild
et al. (2018)
Discounting
A method used to convert future costs
or beneﬁts to present values using a dis-
count rate
Hauschild
et al. (2018)
Disposal
Any operation which is not recovery
even where the operation has as a sec-
ondary consequence the reclamation of
substances or energy
European
Commission
(2008)
Endpoint category
Attribute or aspect of natural envi-
ronment, human health or natural re-
sources, identifying an environmental
issue giving cause for concern
ISO (2006)
Environmental impact
Potential impact on the natural envi-
ronment, human health or the deple-
tion of natural resources, caused by the
interventions between the technosphere
and the ecosphere as covered by LCA
(e.g.
emissions, resource extraction,
land use).
ILCD (2010)
Foreground system
The processes of a system that are spe-
ciﬁc to it
Hauschild
et al. (2018)
Functional unit
Quantiﬁed performance of a product
system for use as a reference unit
ISO (2006)
Hazardous waste
Waste which displays one or more of
the hazardous properties (see Annex III
of the cited document)

Impact category
Class representing environmental issues
of concern to which life cycle inventory
analysis results may be assigned
ISO (2006)
Life
Cycle
Assessment
(LCA)
Compilation and evaluation of the in-
puts, outputs and the potential envi-
ronmental impacts of a product system
throughout its life cycle
ISO (2006)
Life
Cycle
Inventory
(LCI) Analysis
Phase of life cycle assessment involv-
ing the compilation and quantiﬁcation
of inputs and outputs for a product
throughout its life cycle
ISO (2006)
Life Cycle Impact As-
sessment (LCIA)
Phase of life cycle assessment aimed at
understanding and evaluating the mag-
nitude and signiﬁcance of the poten-
tial environmental impacts for a prod-
uct system throughout the life cycle of
the product
ISO (2006)

the secondary material produced

Recovery
Any operation the principal result of
which is waste serving a useful pur-
pose by replacing other materials which
would otherwise have been used to ful-
ﬁl a particular function, or waste being
prepared to fulﬁl that function, in the
plant or in the wider economy
European
Commission
(2008)
Recyclables
Products that can be recycled, e.g. pa-
per, glass, aluminum
Own
deﬁni-
tion
Recycling
Any recovery operation by which waste
materials are reprocessed into prod-
ucts, materials or substances whether
for the original or other purposes. It in-
cludes the reprocessing of organic ma-
terial but does not include energy re-
covery and the reprocessing into mate-
rials that are to be used as fuels or for
backﬁlling operations
European
Commission
(2008)

in a given product system required
to fulﬁl the function expressed by the
functional unit

the eﬀects of the choices made regard-

of a study

mary material is avoided by recycling
(Technological Eﬃciency * Market re-
sponse)

comparable within LCA

==PAGE:187==
Uncertainty analysis
Systematic procedure to quantify the
uncertainty introduced in the results of
a life cycle inventory analysis due to
the cumulative eﬀects of model impre-
cision, input uncertainty and data vari-
ability. Note: Either ranges or proba-
bility distributions are used to deter-
mine uncertainty in the result
ISO (2006)
Waste
Any substance or object which the
holder discards or intends or is required
to discard
European
Commission
(2008)
Waste management
The collection, transport, recovery and
disposal of waste, including the super-
vision of such operations and the after-
care of disposal sites, and including ac-
tions taken as a dealer or broker
European
Commission
(2008)
Waste from Households
(WfH)
UK interpretation of the european
term ‘household waste’. Includes waste
from regular household collection, civic
amenity sites, bulky waste and other
household waste. Excludes waste from
street cleaning/sweeping, gully emp-
tying, separately collected healthcare
waste, soil, Rubble, Plasterboard & As-
bestos waste.
DEFRA
(2019)
Weighting
Converting and possibly aggregating
indicator results across impact cate-
gories using numerical factors based on
value-choices; data prior to weighting
should remain available

et al. (2018)
